<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">CTrain</h1>

<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">This article is about the Calgary, Alberta light rail system. <p><b>CTrain</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">light rail</a> transit system in <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. It began operation in May 25, 1981 and has expanded as the city has increased in population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">[4]</a></sup> The system is operated by <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Transit" title="Calgary Transit">Calgary Transit</a>, as part of the Calgary municipal government's transportation department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> It is now one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_American_light_rail_systems_by_ridership" title="List of North American light rail systems by ridership">busiest light rail transit systems in North America</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup>, with over 300,000 daily riders, and has been growing steadily in recent years. About 45% of workers in downtown Calgary take the CTrain to work.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The CTrain system has two routes, which have been designated Route 201 - the Red line, and Route 202 - the Blue line. they have a combined route length of 59.9 kilometres (37.2 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-figs_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-figs-1">[1]</a></sup> Much of the South leg of the system shares the right of way of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Pacific_Railway" title="Canadian Pacific Railway">Canadian Pacific Railway</a> and there is a connection from the light rail track to the CPR line via a track switch near Heritage Station.</p>
<p>The longer route (Red Line; 35 km long) serves the southern and northwestern areas of the city. The shorter route (Blue Line; 25.7 km long) serves the northeastern and western sections of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Technical-7">[7]</a></sup> Most track is at grade, with its own <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">right-of-way</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Calgary" title="Downtown Calgary">downtown</a> portion is a shared right-of-way, serving both routes along the 7th Avenue South <a href="/wiki/Transit_mall" title="Transit mall">transit mall</a> at street level. This portion is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero-fare" title="Zero-fare">zero-fare</a> zone and serves as a downtown <a href="/wiki/People_mover" title="People mover">people mover</a>. The tracks split at the east and west ends of downtown into lines leading to the south, northeast, west and northwest residential neighbourhoods of Calgary. Six percent of the system is underground, and seven percent is grade-separated (elevated).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Technical-7">[7]</a></sup> Trains are powered by <a href="/wiki/Overhead_line" title="Overhead line">overhead electric wires</a>, using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantograph_(rail)" title="Pantograph (rail)">pantographs</a> to draw power.</p>
<p>In the first quarter of 2015, the CTrain system had an average of 333,800 unlinked passenger trips per weekday, making it <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_American_light_rail_systems_by_ridership" title="List of North American light rail systems by ridership">the busiest light rail system in North America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APTA-Q1-2015_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-APTA-Q1-2015-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APTA-Q4-2013_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-APTA-Q4-2013-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INEGI_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-INEGI-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (November 2016)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup> In 2007, 45% of the people working in downtown Calgary took transit to work; the city's objective is to increase that to 60%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kom_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kom-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Four_car_trains">Four car trains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Four car trains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In late 2015 Calgary Transit began operating four-car LRT trains on the CTrain system. The lengthening of trains was done to alleviate overcrowding as the system was already carrying more than 300,000 passengers per day, and many trains were overcrowded. The lengthening of trains increased the maximum capacity of each train from 600 to 800 passengers, so when enough new LRT cars arrive to lengthen all trains to four cars, the upgrade will increase the LRT system capacity by 33%. Since the platforms on the original stations were designed to only accommodate three-car trains, this required lengthening most of the platforms on the 45 stations on the system and building new electrical substations to power the longer trains. To operate the new four-car trains, the city ordered 63 new cars, although 28 of them were intended to replace the original U2 LRT cars, which have as much as 2.8 million miles on them and are approaching the end of their service lives. Many of the older stations were also worn out by high passenger traffic, and the platforms needed to be rebuilt anyway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The idea of rail transit in Calgary originated in a 1967 Calgary transportation study, which recommended a two-line <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">metro</a> system to enter service in 1978. The original plans had called for two lines:</p>
<ul>
<li>a northwest-to-south line (on a similar routing to the present-day Northwest and South lines) between the original Banff Trail station (at <a href="/wiki/Crowchild_Trail" title="Crowchild Trail">Crowchild Trail</a> and Northland Drive, between the present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brentwood_(C-Train)" title="Brentwood (C-Train)">Brentwood</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dalhousie_(C-Train)" title="Dalhousie (C-Train)">Dalhousie</a> stations) and Southwood station (at Southland Drive, roughly at the location of the present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southland_(C-Train)" title="Southland (C-Train)">Southland station</a>, with five stations in downtown underneath 7 Avenue; and</li>
<li>the west line, which ran from downtown to the community of <a href="/wiki/Glendale,_Calgary" title="Glendale, Calgary">Glendale</a>, primarily along the 26 Avenue SW corridor.</li>
</ul>
<p>A fourth line, a north central line running from downtown to <a href="/wiki/Thorncliffe,_Calgary" title="Thorncliffe, Calgary">Thorncliffe</a> mostly along <a href="/wiki/Centre_Street_(Calgary)" title="Centre Street (Calgary)">Centre Street</a> was also envisioned, but was thought to be beyond the scope of the study.</p>
<p>However, a building boom in the 1970s had caused the heavy rail concept to fall out of favour due to the increased costs of construction, with light rail as its replacement. LRT was chosen over dedicated busways and the expansion of the Blue Arrow bus service (a service similar to <a href="/wiki/Bus_rapid_transit" title="Bus rapid transit">bus rapid transit</a> today) because light rail has lower long-term operating costs and to address traffic congestion problems. The Blue Arrow service all but disappeared in 2000.</p>
<p>The present-day CTrain originated in a 1975 plan, calling for construction of a single line, from the downtown core (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="8 Street Southwest (C-Train)">8 Street station</a>) to Anderson Road (the present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anderson_(C-Train)" title="Anderson (C-Train)">Anderson station</a>). The plan was approved by City Council in May 1977, with construction of what would become the LRT's "South Line" beginning one month later. The South Line opened on May 25, 1981.</p>
<p>Though the South Line was planned to extend to the northwest, political pressures led to the commission of the "Northeast Line", running from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whitehorn_(C-Train)" title="Whitehorn (C-Train)">Whitehorn station</a> (at 36 Street NE and 39 Avenue NE) to the downtown core, with a new downtown terminal station for both lines at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/10_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="10 Street Southwest (C-Train)">10 Street SW</a>, which opened on April 27, 1985.</p>
<p>The "Northwest Line", the extension of the South Line to the city's northwest, was opened on September 17, 1987, in time for the <a href="/wiki/1988_Winter_Olympics" title="1988 Winter Olympics">1988 Winter Olympics</a>. This line ran from the downtown core to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_(C-Train)" title="University (C-Train)">University station</a>, next to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Calgary" title="University of Calgary">University of Calgary</a> campus. Since then, all three lines have been extended incrementally, with most of the stations commissioned and built in the 2000s (with the exception of Brentwood which opened in 1990, three years after the original Northwest line opened):</p>

</center>
<p>The West Line, the extension of the Northeast Line, opened for revenue service on December 10, 2012 as the first new line to open in 25 years. The line runs for 8.2 km from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_West_-_Kerby_(C-Train)" title="Downtown West - Kerby (C-Train)">Downtown West – Kerby station</a> on 7 Avenue at 11 Street SW at the west end of Downtown, westward to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/69_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="69 Street Southwest (C-Train)">69 Street station</a> located at the intersection of 17 Avenue and 69 Street SW.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rolling_stock">Rolling stock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Rolling stock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>The system initially used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siemens-Duewag_U2" title="Siemens-Duewag U2">Siemens-Duewag U2</a> DC LRVs (originally designed for German <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">metros</a>, and used by the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_U-Bahn" title="Frankfurt U-Bahn">Frankfurt U-Bahn</a>. The slightly earlier <a href="/wiki/Edmonton_Light_Rail_Transit" title="Edmonton Light Rail Transit">Edmonton Light Rail Transit</a>, and the slightly later <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Trolley" title="San Diego Trolley">San Diego Trolley</a> were built at approximately the same time and used the same <a href="/wiki/Commercial_off-the-shelf" title="Commercial off-the-shelf">commercial off-the-shelf</a> German LRVs rather than <a href="/wiki/Bespoke" title="Bespoke">custom-designed</a> vehicles such as were used on the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_streetcar_system" title="Toronto streetcar system">Toronto streetcar system</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vancouver_Skytrain" title="Vancouver Skytrain">Vancouver Skytrain</a>. U2 vehicles constituted the entire fleet in Calgary until July 2001, when the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siemens_SD-160" title="Siemens SD-160">Siemens SD-160</a> cars were delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Technical-7">[7]</a></sup> A total of 83 of the original U2 DCs were delivered to Calgary over three separate orders; 27 in 1981, 3 in 1983, and 53 in 1984 and are numbered 2001 – 2083. Today, 80 out of the original 83 U2 DCs remain in service. The success of the first North American LRT systems inspired Siemens to build an LRV plant in <a href="/wiki/Florin,_California" title="Florin, California">Florin, California</a>. Siemens now supplies one-third of North American LRVs and has supplied over 1000 vehicles to 17 North American systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> This will include 258 vehicles for Calgary when the current order of <a href="/wiki/Siemens_S200" title="Siemens S200">Siemens S200</a> vehicles is completed.</p>
<p>The following LRVs have been retired:</p>
<ul>
<li>LRV 2010 was destroyed March 27, 2002 when it collided with a truck at the 4 Avenue SW crossing as it was leaving the downtown. The LRV was retired as a result.</li>
<li>LRV 2019 was damaged in April 2007 when it collided with a flatbed truck in the intersection of Memorial Drive/28 Street SE near Franklin Station. The LRV was retired. However, in 2010 Calgary Transit put together the good end of 2019 with the good end of 2027 to create LRV 2090.</li>
<li>LRV 2050 was damaged in October 2007 when it collided with a vehicle at the 58 Avenue SW crossing near Chinook Station. The LRV was retired as a result. However, in 2010 Calgary Transit decided to repair 2050 and it is now back in service.</li>
<li>LRV 2027 was damaged when it hit a crane in the median of Crowchild Trail near Dalhousie Station in May 2008. The LRV was retired. However, in 2010 Calgary Transit put together the good end of 2019 with the good end of 2027 to create LRV 2090.</li>
<li>LRV 2057 was damaged when it hit a backhoe that was being used in the construction of the new 3 Street W station on 7 Avenue downtown in the summer of 2009. It has been retired.</li>
<li>LRV 2311 was destroyed on September 20, 2016 when it departed Tuscany Station into the tail track, and overshot the end of the rails crashing into the tail fence and a metal power pole at the end of the rails. The LRV was retired as a result.</li>
<li>LRV 2001 was retired on November 18, 2016 as a result of the new <a href="/wiki/Siemens_S200" title="Siemens S200">Siemens S200</a> cars beginning to phase out the outdated <a href="/wiki/Siemens%E2%80%93Duewag_U2" title="Siemens–Duewag U2">Siemens Duewag U2</a> cars.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CTrain_Somerset2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="1016" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/CTrain_Somerset2.jpg/220px-CTrain_Somerset2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/CTrain_Somerset2.jpg/330px-CTrain_Somerset2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/CTrain_Somerset2.jpg/440px-CTrain_Somerset2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CTrain_Somerset2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Somerset–Bridlewood station on the south C-Train line.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1988, the Alberta Government purchased from Siemens two U2 AC units, the first of their kind in North America, for trials on both the Edmonton and Calgary LRT systems. The cars were originally numbered 3001 and 3002 and served in Edmonton from 1988 to Spring 1990. These LRVs came to Calgary in the summer of 1990 and in September, Calgary Transit decided to purchase the cars from the Province and then applied the CT livery to the cars (they were previously plain white in both Edmonton and Calgary). They retained their original fleet numbers of 3001 and 3002 until 1999, when CT renumbered the cars 2101 and 2102. Initially, these two cars were only run together as a two-car consist as they were incompatible with the U2 DCs. In 2003, Calgary Transit made the two U2 ACs compatible as slave cars between two SD160s and have been running them like this ever since.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InteriorCtrain.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4272" data-file-width="2848" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/InteriorCtrain.JPG/170px-InteriorCtrain.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/InteriorCtrain.JPG/255px-InteriorCtrain.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/InteriorCtrain.JPG/340px-InteriorCtrain.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InteriorCtrain.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Interior of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siemens_SD-160" title="Siemens SD-160">Siemens SD-160</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In July 2001, Calgary Transit brought the first of 15 new SD160 LRVs into service to accommodate the South LRT Extension Phase I and increased capacity. Throughout 2003, another 17 SD160 LRVs were introduced into the fleet to accommodate the NW Extension to Dalhousie as well as the South LRT Extension Phase II. However, demand for light rail has exploded in recent years. In the decade prior to 2006, the city's population grew by 25% to over 1 million people, while ridership on the CTrain grew at twice that rate, by 50% in only 10 years. This resulted in severe overcrowding on the trains and demands for better service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guttormson_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guttormson-13">[13]</a></sup> In December 2004, city council approved an order for 33 additional SD-160 vehicles from Siemens to not only address overcrowding, but to accommodate the NE extension to McKnight–Westwinds and the NW extension to Crowfoot. These new SD160s started to enter service in November 2006. In December 2006, CT extended the order by seven cars to a total of 40 cars, which had all been delivered by the spring of 2008. There are now a total of 72 first-generation SD 160s numbered 2201 – 2272.</p>
<p>In November 2007 city council approved purchasing another 38 SD-160 Series 8 LRVs to be used in conjunction with the West LRT extension (2012) and further expansions to the NE (Saddletowne 2012) and NW legs (Tuscany 2014). These are new-generation train cars with lots of upgraded features over the original SD160s. These units started to enter service in December 2010 and are numbered 2301 – 2338. As of May 2012, all have entered revenue service and Calgary's LRV roster now numbers 192.</p>
<p>In September 2013, Calgary Transit ordered 63 S200 LRVs to provide enough cars to run four-car trains, and to retire some of its Siemens-Duewag U2s, which are nearing the end of their useful lifespans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Some of the 80 U2 cars were 34 years old, and all of them had traveled at least 2,000,000 kilometres (1,200,000 mi). The first of the new cars arrived in January, 2016 and delivery was expected take two years. The front of the new cars is customized to resemble a hockey goalie's mask, and they include such new features as heated floors for winter and air conditioning for summer. They also now have high-resolution video cameras covering the entire interior and exterior of the vehicles for security purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 18, 2016 Calgary Transit announced the retirement of the first CTrain purchased, car 2001. Some of the Siemens Duwag U2 cars will be phased out as the new Siemens S200 cars come online. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/></p>
<dl>
<dt>Work cars</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Car# 3275 – shunting/switcher locomotive</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Facts">Facts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Facts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2001, the CTrain became the first public transit system in Canada to claim all of its electricity from emissions-free <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> generation. The electricity is generated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enmax" title="Enmax">Enmax</a> operating in southern Alberta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The trains are powered from the same power grid as before; however, an equivalent amount of electricity is produced at the southern wind farms and "dedicated" to the CTrain. Under Alberta's deregulated market for electricity, large consumers can contract to purchase their electricity from a specific vendor.</p>
<p>On February 18, 2009, Calgary Transit announced that the CTrain had carried one billion riders in the 28 years since the start of service on May 25, 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The trains were now carrying over 269,600 passengers every day, higher than any other light rail system in Canada or the United States. Mayor <a href="/wiki/Dave_Bronconnier" title="Dave Bronconnier">Dave Bronconnier</a> stated that more vehicles were on order to deal with crowding, the northeast and the northwest legs were being extended, and construction of the new west leg was due to start later in the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the following section preliminary timelines for construction of future stations are referenced. For example, construction of a north CTrain line is not expected until after 2023. The city has, on several occasions, accelerated construction of CTrain expansion due to demand and available money. For example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McKnight-Westwinds_(C-Train)" title="McKnight-Westwinds (C-Train)">McKnight–Westwinds</a> station, which opened in 2007, was, as recently as 2002, not planned until beyond 2010. Similarly, the timeline of construction of the south line extension was also pushed up several years due to increasing population and traffic volume. There are plans to develop new routes into the centre north and the southeast of the city.</p>
<p>In 2010, the CCTV Security Camera network was replaced with new, state of the art, IP based cameras along with a new 10GB Fiber-optic network ring to each leg of the LRT line. The project was completed by Contava Inc and will be upgraded as new stations and technology become available.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CTrainIncidentTicket.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/da/CTrainIncidentTicket.jpg/170px-CTrainIncidentTicket.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/da/CTrainIncidentTicket.jpg/255px-CTrainIncidentTicket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/da/CTrainIncidentTicket.jpg/340px-CTrainIncidentTicket.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CTrainIncidentTicket.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A ticket purchased from a C-Train station.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fares">Fares</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fares">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Transit#Fares" title="Calgary Transit">Calgary Transit § Fares</a></div>
<p>Rides taken solely within the downtown are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero-fare" title="Zero-fare">free</a>. This is known as the <i>7th Avenue Free Fare Zone</i> and encompasses all CTrain stations along 7th Avenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Route_details">Route details</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Route details">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calgary_CTrain_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2639" data-file-width="3465" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Calgary_CTrain_Map.png/400px-Calgary_CTrain_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Calgary_CTrain_Map.png/600px-Calgary_CTrain_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Calgary_CTrain_Map.png/800px-Calgary_CTrain_Map.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calgary_CTrain_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Calgary Light Rail System Map</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_CTrain_stations" title="List of CTrain stations">List of CTrain stations</a></div>
<p>There are two major routes in operation: Route 201 running from the far southern to the far northwestern suburbs of Calgary (Somerset/Bridlewood–Tuscany) which has been designated the Red Line, and Route 202 running from the northeastern to the western suburbs (Saddletowne–69 Street SW) which has been designated the Blue Line. The routes merge and share common tracks on the 2 kilometres (1.2 mi) downtown <a href="/wiki/Transit_mall" title="Transit mall">transit mall</a> on 7th Avenue South, which also allows buses and emergency vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Technical-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Downtown_Transit_Mall">Downtown Transit Mall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Downtown Transit Mall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As part of the construction of the original Route 201 South line, nine single-platform stations were built along the 7th Avenue South transit mall, which formed the 7th Avenue <a href="/wiki/Free_public_transport" title="Free public transport">free fare zone</a>. All nine stations opened May 25, 1981 along with the original South Line. The tracks run at grade in a semi-exclusive right of way, shared with buses, city and emergency vehicles. This is a free-fare zone intended to act as a downtown people mover. Fares are only required after trains exit the downtown core.</p>
<p>Westbound stations used to consist of Olympic Plaza (formerly 1 Street E, renamed in 1987), 1 Street W, 4 Street W, and 7 Street W. Eastbound stations consisted of 8 Street W, 6 Street W, 3 Street W, Centre Street and City Hall (formerly 2 Street E, renamed in 1987).</p>
<p>When the Northeast leg opened on April 27, 1985, two stations were added: 3 Street E serving Westbound 202 trains only and 10 Street W, a centre-loading platform, which served as the terminus of both Route 201 and 202, until the Northwest leg opened in 1987, after which it was the terminus for route 202 only.</p>
<p>As part of Calgary's refurbishment project,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> 3 Street E and Olympic Plaza stations have been decommissioned and replaced by the new gateway<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> City Hall station in 2011. 10 Street W was decommissioned and replaced with the Downtown West – Kerby (formerly called 11 Street W) station in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-construction_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-construction-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Downtown_station_refurbishment">Downtown station refurbishment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Downtown station refurbishment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In June 2007, the City of Calgary released information on the schedule for the refurbishment of the remaining original downtown stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The plan involved replacing and relocating most stations, and expanding Centre Street station which was relocated one block east (adjacent to the Telus Convention Centre) in 2000, to board four-car trains. The new stations have retained their existing names (with the exception of 10 Street W becoming Downtown West – Kerby in 2012); however, they may be shifted one block east or west, or to the opposite side of 7th Avenue. The refurbishment project was completed on December 8, 2012, when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_West_-_Kerby_(C-Train)" title="Downtown West - Kerby (C-Train)">Downtown West – Kerby</a> station was opened to the public in conjunction with the West LRT opening event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>1 Street W – new platform relocated one block east opened October 28, 2005.</li>
<li>7 Street W – new platform relocated one block east opened February 27, 2009.</li>
<li>6 Street W – reconstructed in original location. Original platform closed April 7, 2008 and new platform opened March 27, 2009.</li>
<li>8 Street W – new platform relocated one block east opened December 18, 2009.</li>
<li>3 Street W – reconstructed in original location. Original platform closed April 20, 2009 and new platform opened March 12, 2010.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3_Street_Southeast_(C-Train)" title="3 Street Southeast (C-Train)">3 Street E</a> – permanently closed May 3, 2010. Replaced by new dual-platform City Hall Station opening July 6, 2011.</li>
<li>4 Street W – reconstructed in original location. Original platform closed January 7, 2010 and new platform opened January 21, 2011.</li>
<li>City Hall – original Eastbound platform rebuilt with new Westbound platform to replace 3 Street E and Olympic Plaza. Original platform closed May 3, 2010 and new dual-platform station opened July 6, 2011. Olympic Plaza was closed permanently at this time. Eastbound platform re-closed following the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Stampede" title="Calgary Stampede">2011 Stampede</a> to finish construction and officially opened September 19, 2011.</li>
<li>Olympic Plaza – permanently closed July 6, 2011. Replaced by new dual-platform City Hall Station.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/10_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="10 Street Southwest (C-Train)">10 Street W</a> – permanently closed and removed on September 15, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-construction_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-construction-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The new station replacing it, which opened on December 8, 2012, has dual side-loading platforms and is located one block west. This project was initially proposed to be undertaken in 2006, following the opening of the new 1 Street W station. However, the City of Calgary decided to defer the project to coincide with the opening of the West Line and continue on with refurbishment of the other stations. This new station was initially called "11 Street W" up until the Summer of 2012 when it was renamed to Downtown West – Kerby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>This required that the stations be closed during demolition and reconstruction. The new stations feature longer platforms for longer trains, better integration of the platforms into the sidewalk system, better lighting, and more attractive landscaping and street furniture. This project was shortlisted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> for the New/Old category in the 2012 <a href="/wiki/World_Architecture_Festival" title="World Architecture Festival">World Architecture Festival</a> in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Route_201_-_Red_Line">Route 201 - Red Line</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Route 201 - Red Line">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This route comprises two legs connected by the downtown transit mall: the South leg (17.3 kilometres (10.7 mi)) and the Northwest leg (15.7 kilometres (9.8 mi)). There are eleven stations on the South leg and nine on the Northwest leg. Total length of the line: 33 kilometres (21 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Technical-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_leg">South leg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: South leg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This was the first leg of the system to be built. Seven stations on this leg opened on May 25, 1981, as the first light railway line to serve the city. From north to south, they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_Park/Stampede_(C-Train)" title="Victoria Park/Stampede (C-Train)">Victoria Park/Stampede</a> (renamed from Stampede in 1995), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erlton/Stampede_(C-Train)" title="Erlton/Stampede (C-Train)">Erlton/Stampede</a> (renamed from Erlton in 1995), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/39_Avenue_(C-Train)" title="39 Avenue (C-Train)">39 Avenue</a> (renamed from 42 Avenue in 1986), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinook_(C-Train)" title="Chinook (C-Train)">Chinook</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heritage_(C-Train)" title="Heritage (C-Train)">Heritage</a> (also the site of the Haysboro LRT Storage Facility), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southland_(C-Train)" title="Southland (C-Train)">Southland</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anderson_(C-Train)" title="Anderson (C-Train)">Anderson</a> (also the site of the Anderson LRT Yards). The original South line was 10.9 km long. On October 9, 2001, the line was extended south 3.4 km and two new stations were added: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canyon_Meadows_(C-Train)" title="Canyon Meadows (C-Train)">Canyon Meadows</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_Creek-Lacombe_(C-Train)" title="Fish Creek-Lacombe (C-Train)">Fish Creek–Lacombe</a>, as part of the South LRT Extension Phase I. On June 28, 2004, Phase II opened adding 3 km of track and two more stations: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shawnessy_(C-Train)" title="Shawnessy (C-Train)">Shawnessy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somerset-Bridlewood_(C-Train)" title="Somerset-Bridlewood (C-Train)">Somerset–Bridlewood</a>. A further three stations – Silverado (most likely in the area of 194th Avenue SW), 212th Avenue South, and Pine Creek (in the area around 228th Avenue SW) – are planned once the communities adjacent to their location are developed, likely beyond 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 2012 RouteAhead<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> report, the extension of the South Line to 210th Avenue is considered to be a medium-term priority as communities adjacent to that area are constructed. With an estimated cost of $180 million and an estimated annual operating cost of $7 million the 3.5 km extension is estimated to serve 8 million passenger rides annually.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northwest_leg">Northwest leg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Northwest leg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This was the third leg of the system to be built. Five stations on this leg opened on September 7, 1987. From the most central to the most northwesterly, they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunnyside_(C-Train)" title="Sunnyside (C-Train)">Sunnyside</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SAIT/ACAD/Jubilee_(C-Train)" title="SAIT/ACAD/Jubilee (C-Train)">SAIT/ACAD/Jubilee</a> (the station name in full is "<a href="/wiki/Southern_Alberta_Institute_of_Technology" title="Southern Alberta Institute of Technology">Southern Alberta Institute of Technology</a>/<a href="/wiki/Alberta_College_of_Art_and_Design" title="Alberta College of Art and Design">Alberta College of Art and Design</a>/<a href="/wiki/Southern_Alberta_Jubilee_Auditorium" title="Southern Alberta Jubilee Auditorium">Jubilee Auditorium</a>"), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lions_Park_(C-Train)" title="Lions Park (C-Train)">Lions Park</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banff_Trail_(C-Train)" title="Banff Trail (C-Train)">Banff Trail</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_(C-Train)" title="University (C-Train)">University</a>. The original Northwest leg was 5.6 km long. On August 31, 1990, the line was extended 1 km and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brentwood_(C-Train)" title="Brentwood (C-Train)">Brentwood</a> station was opened as the new terminus. On December 15, 2003, the line was extended 3 km again and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dalhousie_(C-Train)" title="Dalhousie (C-Train)">Dalhousie</a> station was opened. On June 15, 2009, the line was extended 3.6 km and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crowfoot_(C-Train)" title="Crowfoot (C-Train)">Crowfoot</a> (formerly Crowfoot-Centennial) was opened. It was extended further by 2.5 km to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuscany_(C-Train)" title="Tuscany (C-Train)">Tuscany</a> Station on August 25, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCMinutes20071106_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-CCMinutes20071106-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Route_202_-_Blue_Line">Route 202 - Blue Line</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Route 202 - Blue Line">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This route is composed of two legs connected by the downtown transit mall: the Northeast leg (15.5 kilometres (9.6 mi)) and the newer West leg (8.2 kilometres (5.1 mi)). The Northeast leg has ten stations and the West leg has six stations. Total length of this route: 25.7 kilometres (16.0 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Technical-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northeast_leg">Northeast leg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Northeast leg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This was the second leg of the system to be built. Seven stations opened on April 27, 1985, from downtown to the northeast. They are: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bridgeland/Memorial_(C-Train)" title="Bridgeland/Memorial (C-Train)">Bridgeland/Memorial</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoo_(C-Train)" title="Zoo (C-Train)">Zoo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barlow/Max_Bell_(C-Train)" title="Barlow/Max Bell (C-Train)">Barlow/Max Bell</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_(C-Train)" title="Franklin (C-Train)">Franklin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marlborough_(C-Train)" title="Marlborough (C-Train)">Marlborough</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rundle_(C-Train)" title="Rundle (C-Train)">Rundle</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whitehorn_(C-Train)" title="Whitehorn (C-Train)">Whitehorn</a>. The original Northeast line was 9.8 km long. On December 17, 2007, the line was extended 2.8 km further north to an eighth station – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McKnight%E2%80%93Westwinds_(C-Train)" title="McKnight–Westwinds (C-Train)">McKnight–Westwinds</a>. On August 27, 2012, another 2.9 km extension of track opened and added two more stations – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martindale_(C-Train)" title="Martindale (C-Train)">Martindale</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saddletowne_(C-Train)" title="Saddletowne (C-Train)">Saddletowne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCMinutes20071106_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-CCMinutes20071106-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007WestLRT_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-2007WestLRT-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Additional stations are proposed for development, likely beyond 2023, at 96th Avenue, <a href="/wiki/Country_Hills_Boulevard" title="Country Hills Boulevard">Country Hills Boulevard</a>, 128th Avenue (north of Skyview Ranch) and <a href="/wiki/Stoney_Trail" title="Stoney Trail">Stoney Trail</a> (in the Stonegate Landing development),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007WestLRT_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-2007WestLRT-36">[36]</a></sup> as those areas are developed for future LRT infrastructure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_leg">West leg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: West leg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This was the fourth leg of the system to be built, although it was included in the original plans for the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> It was built last because it was anticipated to have lower ridership and higher construction costs than the previous legs. Construction of the 8.2 kilometre<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> (5 mile) leg began in 2009. It opened on December 10, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The City of Calgary began a review process in late 2006 to update the plans to current standards, and Calgary City Council gave final approval to the project<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and allocated the required $566-million project funding on November 20, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCMinutes20071106_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-CCMinutes20071106-34">[34]</a></sup> Funding for the project was sourced from the infrastructure fund that was created when the Province of Alberta returned the education tax portion of property taxes to the city. Construction of this leg began in 2009. It was constructed at the same time as further extensions of the NE and NW lines of the LRT system that were approved in November 2007.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CTrain_SW_construction1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="2024" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/CTrain_SW_construction1.jpg/220px-CTrain_SW_construction1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/CTrain_SW_construction1.jpg/330px-CTrain_SW_construction1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/CTrain_SW_construction1.jpg/440px-CTrain_SW_construction1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CTrain_SW_construction1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
West LRT construction over <a href="/wiki/Bow_Trail" title="Bow Trail">Bow Trail</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The West LRT leg<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> has six stations (from east to west): <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunalta_(C-Train)" title="Sunalta (C-Train)">Sunalta</a> (near 16th Street SW), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaganappi_Point_(C-Train)" title="Shaganappi Point (C-Train)">Shaganappi Point</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westbrook_(C-Train)" title="Westbrook (C-Train)">Westbrook</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/45_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="45 Street Southwest (C-Train)">45 Street Southwest</a> (Westgate), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sirocco_(C-Train)" title="Sirocco (C-Train)">Sirocco</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/69_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="69 Street Southwest (C-Train)">69 Street Southwest</a> (west of 69th Street near Westside Recreation Centre).</p>
<p>The updated alignment from the 2007 West LRT Report<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007WestLRT_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-2007WestLRT-36">[36]</a></sup> includes the line running on an elevated guideway beginning west of the Downtown West – Kerby Station, running along the CPR right of way to Bow Trail SW, and then to 24th Street SW. The line then runs at grade past Shaganappi Point Station and drops into a tunnel to 33rd Street SW. The tunnel then runs under the <a href="/wiki/Westbrook_Mall" title="Westbrook Mall">Westbrook Mall</a> parking lot, and the former site of the now-demolished Ernest Manning Senior High School. The line then follows the north side of 17th Avenue SW past 37th Street SW below grade to 45 Street Station. Past 45th Street the line runs at grade, and approaching Sarcee Trail SW moves onto an elevated guideway that passes over the freeway. The line then runs at grade to Sirocco Station, then proceeds to drop below grade and pass under eastbound 17th Avenue SW at 69th Street SW and return to grade on the south side of the avenue. The line then terminates at 69 Street Station located to the west of 69th Street SW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West_LRT_Map_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-West_LRT_Map-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three of the new West leg stations are located at grade. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westbrook_(C-Train)" title="Westbrook (C-Train)">Westbrook</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/45_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="45 Street Southwest (C-Train)">45 Street SW</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/69_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="69 Street Southwest (C-Train)">69 Street SW</a> stations are located below grade, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunalta_(C-Train)" title="Sunalta (C-Train)">Sunalta</a> is an elevated station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> On October 5, 2009, the city council announced approval of a plan to put a portion of the West leg into a trench at 45th Street and 17th Avenue SW, a move welcomed by advocates who fought to have it run underground. The change cost an estimated $61 million; however, lower-than-expected construction costs were expected to absorb much of the change.</p>
<p>The cost for the project is, however, <a href="/wiki/Cost_overrun" title="Cost overrun">over budget</a> by at least <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">C$</a>35 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and the overall cost could be more than <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">C$</a>1.46 billion because of soaring costs of land used and the integration of public art into the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The public art aspect of the project was neglected in its initial form. Because City Hall regulations for big construction projects require incorporation of public art, City Hall had to find the money. Therefore, the West LRT project cost C$8.6 million more than expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 29, 2009 city council announced that the contract to construct the West LRT had been awarded to a consortium led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SNC_Lavalin" title="SNC Lavalin">SNC Lavalin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Future extension of the West leg to Aspen Woods Station (around 17th Avenue and 85th Street SW) has been planned, and future extensions further west to 101st Street SW may be added as new communities adjacent to 17th Avenue SW are built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West_LRT_Map_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-West_LRT_Map-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 15, 2012, testing of the leg began with two LRT cars. As the construction of the leg moved towards completion, four LRT cars were used, until revenue service began on December 10, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_lines">Future lines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Future lines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For the Red Line, in its 30-year RouteAhead plan, the South line may be extended another 3.5 km to a possible 210 Avenue SW station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> For the Blue Line, from the same plan, there are more possible extensions to the northeast to either the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_International_Airport" title="Calgary International Airport">Calgary International Airport</a> (via a spur line),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extension_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extension-59">[59]</a></sup> or to 128 Avenue NE, or to have both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Future_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Future-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are plans to build an additional line to the southeast from the city centre. Calgary Transit has drafted a plan for a transit-only right-of-way, known as the SETWAY (South East Transit Way) for the interim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calgarytransit.com_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-calgarytransit.com-61">[61]</a></sup> A second, northern line is to be planned beyond 2023 but the alignment is still pending.</p>
<p>As for a possible underground leg in downtown (under 8 Avenue South), the cost of the project will be at least C$800 million (in 2012 dollars), but its priority has been lowered because there is no funding available for it. However, the overall cost of this and other projects could be at least C$8 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extension_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Extension-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Future_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Future-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Route_203_-_Green_Line">Route 203 - Green Line</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Route 203 - Green Line">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>This proposed future route would cross the downtown core at right angles to the downtown transit mall and connect two new legs: the Southeast leg and the North-Central leg. It would exceed the capacity of the downtown transit mall, requiring that it use a new right of way going over or under the existing transit mall. Elevated tracks would conflict with Calgary's downtown <a href="/wiki/%2B15" title="+15">+15</a> system, which is the most extensive pedestrian skywalk system in the world, so this option is unlikely. Most likely the system will go underground, crossing underneath the future downtown subway, which already has a short section of tunnel built under <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Avenue" title="Stephen Avenue">8th Avenue S</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Ghost_station" title="Ghost station">ghost station</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Municipal_Building" title="Calgary Municipal Building">Calgary Municipal Building</a>.The exact routes and station locations are currently in the planning stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The City of Calgary has already committed a third of the money for the Green Line, and the federal government has promised an additional third, but provincial funding for the remaining third of the project must be secured by October 2016, or construction of the Green Line might have to be broken up into stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_leg">North leg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: North leg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This leg of the Green Line would serve the residential communities of Country Hills, Coventry Hills, Harvest Hills, Panorama Hills, and other communities, possibly in the future extending as far as the nearby <a href="/wiki/Airdrie,_Alberta" title="Airdrie, Alberta">City of Airdrie</a>. The Green Line North, as it has been re-designated, will be a mix of grade level and underground infrastructure extending north from the downtown core along <a href="/wiki/Centre_Street_(Calgary)" title="Centre Street (Calgary)">Centre Street North</a>.</p>
<p>In July 2015, the Canadian federal government committed to put $1.5 billion into funding the Green Line LRT or one-third of the project's $4.6 billion cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Discussions between the city of Calgary and the province continue with the goal of building the new light rail line instead of developing a BRT system as an interim measure.</p>
<p>In January, 2015, Calgary City Council approved the Green Line North (formerly known as North Central LRT), setting Centre Street N. as the route. In December, 2015 Council approved the planning report on Green Line funding, staging, and delivery. The North leg is expected to be the first section of the Green Line to be built. Actual completion dates will depend on delivery of promised federal and provincial government funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_leg">Southeast leg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Southeast leg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This leg is planned to run from downtown (although on a different routing, not following the 7th Avenue corridor) to the communities of Douglasdale and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McKenzie_Lake" title="McKenzie Lake">McKenzie Lake</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McKenzie_Towne" title="McKenzie Towne">McKenzie Towne</a> in the southeast, and onwards past Highway 22X into the so-called "Homesteads" region east of the Deerfoot Trail extension.</p>
<p>Eighteen stations have been planned for this route and the project is expected to be completely built by 2039.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SELRT_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-SELRT-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three of the proposed downtown stations are expected to be built underground,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and the rest of the line will follow the 52 Street SE corridor from Douglasdale and McKenzie Towne to <a href="/wiki/Auburn_Bay,_Calgary" title="Auburn Bay, Calgary">Auburn Bay</a> (south of Highway 22X) and then wind its way through Health Campus (adjacent to the southeast hospital) and <a href="/wiki/Seton,_Calgary" title="Seton, Calgary">Seton</a>. Unlike Routes 201 and 202, which use high-floor U2 and SD-160 LRVs, the eastern route is expected to employ <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_floor" title="Low floor">low-floor</a> LRVs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> such as the Bombardier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flexity_Outlook_(Toronto_LRT_car)" title="Flexity Outlook (Toronto LRT car)">Flexity Freedom</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Siemens_S70" title="Siemens S70">Siemens S70</a>.</p>
<p>From north to south, the proposed stations are: Eau Claire, Central (at 6 Avenue), Macleod Trail, 4 Street SE, Ramsay/Inglewood, Crossroads, Highfield, Lynnwood, Ogden, South Hill, Quarry Park, Douglasglen, Shepard, Prestwick, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McKenzie_Towne" title="McKenzie Towne">McKenzie Towne</a>, Auburn Bay/Mahogany (at 52nd Street), Health Campus/Seton (the station likely will share the name of the hospital and expected to be completed by 2039),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SELRT_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-SELRT-66">[66]</a></sup> with further stations to the south expected in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SELRT2004_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-SELRT2004-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Construction of the South East LRT would cost over C$2.7 billion over 27 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SELRT_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-SELRT-66">[66]</a></sup> Because there was no funding available, the city laid out plans to build a transit way for the South East BRT known as SETWAY. Open houses to explore the idea of a transit way for the South East occurred in the South East communities of Ramsay, Riverbend and McKenzie Towne in January 2012. Between 1999 and 2006 Calgary Transit conducted studies for the South East LRT to find ways to make improvements of overall transit use in the South East for short term while having LRT being the long-term goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calgarytransit.com_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-calgarytransit.com-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 3 2016, it was announced that an additional C$250 million in additional funding was allocated in a joint venture by the Federal and Provincial Governments. This comes in line with a possible final cost estimate of the South East LRT to be announced in March 2017.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spur_line_to_Calgary_International_Airport">Spur line to Calgary International Airport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Spur line to Calgary International Airport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As part of the C$8 billion, 30-year <a class="external text" href="http://www.routeahead.ca/" rel="nofollow">RouteAhead</a> plan, there is a plan to connect downtown Calgary with <a href="/wiki/Calgary_International_Airport" title="Calgary International Airport">Calgary International Airport</a> via a higher order transit connection, which may take an initial form as a Route 202 <a href="/wiki/Branch_line" title="Branch line">spur line</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extension_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Extension-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Future_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Future-60">[60]</a></sup> The <a class="external text" href="http://www.calgary.ca/Transportation/TI/Pages/Road-projects/Airport-Trail-Tunnel.aspx" rel="nofollow">Airport Trail tunnel</a> was built with room to accommodate a future two-track CTrain <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">right-of-way</a>. This project was opened on May 25, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_future_improvements">Other future improvements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other future improvements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In late 2015, Calgary Transit completed upgrading its entire system to operate four-car trains instead of the original three-car trains. When enough new LRVs are delivered to lengthen all trains to four cars, this will increase the rush-hour capacity of the system by 33%. By 2023, Calgary Transit also plans to begin decommissioning some of the original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siemens-Duewag_U2" title="Siemens-Duewag U2">Siemens-Duewag U2s</a> (as of 2010 80 of the original 83 were in use, and nearing 29 years of service, by 2023 they will be 42 years old). Calgary Transit has ordered some 60 new <a href="/wiki/Siemens_S200" title="Siemens S200">Siemens S200</a> LRV cars to replace 28 of the existing U2s in addition to lengthening many of the trains to four cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_underground_infrastructure">Further underground infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Further underground infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to numerous tunnels to allow trains to pass under roadways, geographic features, and mainline railways, there are other notable underground portions of Calgary's CTrain system.</p>
<p>Part of the system through downtown is planned to be transferred underground when needed to maintain reliable service. Given this, portions of the needed infrastructure have been built as adjacent and associated land was developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> As a result of this original plan, when the City of Calgary built a new Municipal Building, it built a short section of tunnel to connect the existing CPR tunnel to the future tunnel under 8th Avenue S. The turnoff to this station is visible in the tunnel on the Red Line entering downtown from the south, shortly before City Hall. However, after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_explorer" title="Urban explorer">urban explorers</a> discovered the tunnel and visited it during a transit strike,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the city walled off the spur tunnel with concrete blocks.</p>
<p>As the population of metropolitan Calgary increases and growing suburbs require new lines and extensions, the higher train volumes will exceed the ability of the downtown section along 7th Avenue S to accommodate them. To provide for long-term expansion, the city is reviewing its plans to put parts of the downtown section underground. The current plans allow the expanded Blue Line (Northeast/West) to use the existing 7th Avenue S surface infrastructure. The expanded Red Line (Northwest/South), now sharing 7th Avenue S with the Blue Line, will be relocated to a new tunnel dug beneath 8th Avenue S. The future Southeast/Downtown route will probably enter downtown through a shorter tunnel under one or more streets (candidates include 2nd Street W, 5th Street W, 6th Street W, 8th Avenue S, 10th Avenue S, 11th Avenue S, and 12th Avenue S). Although Calgary City Council commissioned a functional study for the downtown metro component of the CTrain system in November 2007, the city is unlikely to complete this expansion before 2017 unless additional funding is received from provincial or federal governments. The cost of bringing the potential underground leg under 8 Avenue South could be at least C$800 million, according to Calgary Transit's 30-year <a class="external text" href="http://www.routeahead.ca/" rel="nofollow">RouteAhead</a> plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extension_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-Extension-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Future_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-Future-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="CTrain_stations">CTrain stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: CTrain stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_CTrain_stations" title="List of CTrain stations">List of CTrain stations</a></div>
<p>The typical CTrain station outside the downtown core allows for several methods of passenger arrival and departure. Many CTrain passengers travel to and from suburban stations on feeder bus routes that wind their way through surrounding neighbourhoods. Another popular option is a <a href="/wiki/Park_and_ride" title="Park and ride">Park and Ride</a> lot, in which commuters drive to a station by car and then transfer to a CTrain to complete their journey. Alternatively, some CTrain passengers disembark at drop-off zones from vehicles travelling elsewhere; because many of these commuters are conveyed by their spouses, these zones are branded as <a href="/wiki/Park_and_ride#Kiss_and_ride" title="Park and ride">Kiss and Ride</a> areas.</p>

<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/6_Street_Southwest/7_Street_Southwest_(C-Train)" title="6 Street Southwest/7 Street Southwest (C-Train)">6th/7th St. SW</a></td>
<td>Red &amp; Blue Line</td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ghost_station">Ghost station</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ghost station">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ghost_station" title="Ghost station">Ghost station</a></div>
<table class="wikitable sortable">
<tr>
<th>Station</th>
<th>Route</th>
<th>Area</th>
<th>Open</th>
<th>Location</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Calgary_Municipal_Building" title="Calgary Municipal Building">Calgary Municipal Building</a></td>
<td>Route 201</td>
<td>Downtown</td>
<td>Future</td>
<td>Planned subway line under 8th Ave</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>This underground station was built under the Municipal Building as part of a proposed future 8th Avenue subway. There is a short section of unused transit tunnel leading to it from the South leg that was blocked off after underground <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_explorers" title="Urban explorers">urban explorers</a> started following it into the station.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ridership">Ridership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ridership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The CTrain's high ridership rate and cost effectiveness can be attributed to a number of factors. The nature of Calgary itself has encouraged CTrain use. Calgary has grown into the second largest head office city in Canada, with a very dense downtown business district. Most of the head offices are crowded into about 1 square kilometre (250 acres) of land in the downtown core. In the last half century the population of Calgary has grown dramatically, outpacing the ability of roads to transport people into the city centre, while the central business district has grown up vertically rather than spread out into the suburbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubbel_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbel-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, Calgary residents, particularly its influential inner city community associations, voted against proposals to build major freeways into its city centre, forcing new commuters to use transit as their numbers increased while downtown street and freeway capacity remained the same. City planners limited the number of parking spaces in the downtown core since the narrow downtown streets could not allow more traffic to park. At the same time, Calgary's maturation as a globally influential head office city caused many surface parking lots to be replaced by new skyscrapers, which increased office workers while reducing parking spaces. This eventually made it prohibitively expensive for most people to park downtown. The shortage of downtown parking caused fees to become among the most expensive in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> As a result, in 2012 50% of Calgary's 120,000 downtown workers used Calgary Transit to get to work, with a long-term goal of reaching that mark to 60% of downtown workers in the future.</p>
<p>Forward planning for the CTrain played an important role. Although the light rail system was not chosen until 1976, the city planners had proactively reserved transit corridors for some form of high capacity transport during the 1960s, and the right-of-ways for the system were reserved when Calgary's population was less than 500,000, whereas today it is well over twice that number. <a href="/wiki/Bus_rapid_transit" title="Bus rapid transit">Bus rapid transit</a> lines were put in place along future routes to increase commuter numbers prior to constructing proposed future LRT lines. Rather than demolishing buildings, the city reached an agreement with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CP_Rail" title="CP Rail">CP Rail</a> to build most of the south line in available space inside an existing CPR right-of-way. Large parts of the other lines were built in the medians and along the edges of freeways and other major roads. Automobile driver objections were muted by adding extra lanes to roads for cars at the same time as putting in the LRT tracks, which reduced costs for both, and by adding grade-separating intersections which reduced both driver and train delays. The lines and stations were placed to serve large outlying suburbs and the central and other business districts, and to serve existing and predicted travel patterns.</p>
<p>Costs were controlled during construction and operation of the system by going with the lowest bidder and using relatively cheap, commercially available technology without regard for "buy Canadian" policies. This has worked out well for a pioneer system because the German technology chosen has since become a more or less standard design for most North American LRT systems, and compatible new-generation equipment with new features is available off-the-shelf. A grade-separated system was passed over in preference for a system with few elevated or buried segments, and the trains and stations selected were of the tried and tested, utilitarian variety (for example, vehicles were not air conditioned, storage yards were not automated, and stations were usually modest concrete platforms with a shelter overhead). This allowed greater amounts of track to be laid within available budgets. The CTrain reduced fare collection costs by using an honour system of payment. Transit police check passenger tickets at random, and fines are set at a level high enough that those who are caught pay the costs for those who evade detection. Staffing costs were kept low by employing a minimum number of workers, and because the system is all-electric (wind powered) it can run all night with only 1 driver per train and 2 people in the control room. It now runs 22 hours per day without significantly increased overhead. (The other 2 hours are reserved for track maintenance).</p>
<p>Although not universally grade separated, the CTrain is able to operate at high speeds on much of its track because it is separated from traffic and pedestrians by fences and concrete <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollards" title="Bollards">bollards</a>. The downtown 7th Avenue transit way is limited to trains, buses, and emergency vehicles, with private cars prohibited. Trains are given priority right of way at most road crossings outside of downtown. As a result, trains are able to operate at 80 km/h (50 mph) outside of downtown, and 40 km/h (25 mph) along the 7th Avenue corridor. 7th Avenue is a free fare zone, intended as a downtown people-mover to encourage use for short hops through the downtown core. The city manages to achieve very high transit capacity on the 7th Avenue transit corridor by staging the traffic lights, so that all the trains move forward in unison to the next station on the synchronized green lights, and load and unload passengers on the intervening red lights. The trains are now 1 block long, but buses occupy the empty gaps every second block between trains and the buses unload and load passengers while the trains pass them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capital_Utilization_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Capital_Utilization-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, the U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Accounting_Office" title="General Accounting Office">General Accounting Office</a> released a study of the cost-effectiveness of American light rail systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GAO_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-GAO-79">[79]</a></sup> Although not included in the report, Calgary had a capital cost of US$24.5 million per mile (year 2000 dollars), which would be the sixth lowest (Edmonton was given as US$41.7 million per mile). Because of its high ridership (then 188,000 boardings per weekday, now over 300,000) the capital cost per passenger was $2,400 per daily passenger, by far the lowest of the 14 systems compared, while the closest American system was Sacramento at $9,100 per weekday passenger). Operating costs are also low, in 2005, the CTrain cost CDN$163 per operating hour to operate. With an average of 600 boardings per hour, in 2001 cost per LRT passenger was CDN$0.27, compared to $1.50 for bus passengers in Calgary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capital_Utilization_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Capital_Utilization-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Facilities">Facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CTrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Anderson Garage – LRV indoor storage and training facilities</li>
<li>Haysboro Garage – small indoor and outdoor LRV storage; LRV yard and Turner Storage Area</li>
<li>Oliver Bowen Maintenance Centre – major LRV repair and shops; storage for 60 cars (and up to 108 cars after expansion)</li>
</ul>
